0 0 1 1.167 0m7.355 1.705c-.31-.461-.746-.758-1.23-.837a1.44 1.44 0 0 0-1.11.275c-.312.24-.505.543-.59.881a1.74 1.74 0 0 0-.906-.465 1.47 1.47 0 0 0-.82.106l-2.182-2.182a1.56 1.56 0 0 0-2.2 0 1.54 1.54 0 0 0-.396.701 1.56 1.56 0 0 0-2.21-.01 1.55 1.55 0 0 0-.416.753c-.624-.624-1.649-.624-2.237-.037a1.557 1.557 0 0 0 0 2.2c-.239.1-.501.238-.715.453a1.56 1.56 0 0 0 0 2.2l.516.515a1.556 1.556 0 0 0-.753 2.615L7.01 19c1.32 1.319 2.909 2.189 4.475 2.449q.482.08.971.08c.85 0 1.653-.198 2.393-.579.231.033.46.054.686.054 1.266 0 2.457-.52 3.505-1.567 2.763-2.763 2.552-5.734 1.439-7.586z" clip-rule="evenodd"></path></svg></div></div></div></a></span></div><div class="pw-multi-vote-count l li lj lk ll lm ln lo"><p class="bf b dv z du"><span class="lp">--</span></p></div></div></div><div><div class="bm" aria-hidden="false"><button class="ao la lq lr ab q fk ls lt" aria-label="responses"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" viewBox="0 0 24 24" class="lu"><path d="M18.006 16.803c1.533-1.456 2.234-3.325 2.234-5.321C20.24 7.357 16.709 4 12.191 4S4 7.357 4 11.482c0 4.126 3.674 7.482 8.191 7.482.817 0 1.622-.111 2.393-.327.231.2.48.391.744.559 1.06.693 2.203 1.044 3.399 1.044.224-.008.4-.112.486-.287a.49.49 0 0 0-.042-.518c-.495-.67-.845-1.364-1.04-2.057a4 4 0 0 1-.125-.598zm-3.122 1.055-.067-.223-.315.096a8 ao ap ex mf mg lt mh mi mj mk ml s mm mn mo mp mq mr ms u mt mu mv"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" fill="none" viewBox="0 0 24 24"><path fill="currentColor" .777-.416l4.599 3.066a.5.5 0 0 1 0 .832" clip-rule="evenodd"></path></svg><div class="j i d"><p class="bf b bg z du">Listen</p></div></button></div></div></div></div></div></div></div></div><div class="bm" aria-hidden="false" aria-describedby="postFooterSocialMenu" aria-labelledby="postFooterSocialMenu"><div><div class="bm" aria-hidden="false"><button aria-controls="postFooterSocialMenu" aria-expanded="false" aria-label="Share Post" data-testid="headerSocialShareButton" class="af fk ah ai aj ak al me an ao ap ex mf mg lt mh mi mj mk ml s mm mn mo mp mq mr ms u mt mu mv"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="24" height="24" fill="none" viewBox="0 0 24 24"><path fill="currentColor" fill-rule="evenodd" d="M15.218 4.931a.4.4 0 0 1-.118.132l.012.006a.45.45 0 0 1-.292.074.5.5 0 0 1-.3-.13l-2.02-2.02v7.07c0 .28-.23.5-.5.5s-.5-.22-.5-.5v-7.04l-2 2a.45.45 0 0 1-.57.04h-.02a.4.4 0 0 1-.16-.3.4.4 0 0 1 .1-.32l2.8-2.8a.5.5 0 0 1 .7 0l2.8 2.79a.42.42 0 0 1 .068.498m-.106.138.008.004v-.01zM16 7.063h1.5a2 2 0 0 1 2 2v10a2 2 0 0 1-2 2h-11c-1.1 0-2-.9-2-2v-10a2 2 0 0 1 2-2H8a.5.5 0 0 1 .35.15.5.5 0 0 1 .15.35.5.5 0 0 1-.15.35.5.5 0 0 1-.35.15H6.4c-.5 0-.9.4-.9.9v10.2a.9.9 0 0 0 .9.9h11.2c.5 0 .9-.4.9-.9v-10.2c0-.5-.4-.9-.9-.9H16a.5.5 0 0 1 0-1" clip-rule="evenodd"></path></svg><div class="j i d"><p class="bf b bg z du">Share</p></div></button></div></div></div></div></div></div></div></div></div><p id="9da0" class="pw-post-body-paragraph mw mx gu my b mz na nb nc nd ne nf ng nh ni nj nk nl nm nn no np nq nr ns nt gn bk">In<a class="af nu" rel="noopener ugc nofollow" target="_blank" href="/adcs-attack-paths-in-bloodhound-part-1-799f3d3b03cf"> Part 1</a> of this series, we explained how we incorporated Active Directory Certificate Services (ADCS) objects into <a class="af nu" href="https://github.com/SpecterOps/BloodHound" rel="noopener ugc nofollow" target="_blank">BloodHound</a> and demonstrated how to effectively use BloodHound to identify attack paths, including the ESC1 domain escalation technique. <a class="af nu" rel="noopener ugc nofollow" target="_blank" href="/adcs-attack-paths-in-bloodhound-part-2-ac7f925d1547">Part 2</a> covered the Golden Certificates and the ESC3 techniques.</p><p id="ad46" class="pw-post-body-paragraph mw mx gu my b mz na nb nc nd ne nf ng nh ni nj nk nl nm nn no np nq nr ns nt gn bk">In this blog post, we will continue to explore more of the new edges we have introduced with ADCS support in BloodHound. More specifically, we will cover how we have incorporated the ESC6, ESC9, and ESC10 domain escalation techniques.</p><p id="680f" class="pw-post-body-paragraph mw mx gu my b mz na nb nc nd ne nf ng nh ni nj nk nl nm nn no np nq nr ns nt gn bk"><a class="af nu" href="https://www.keyfactor.com/author-results/?authors=keyfactor-team" rel="noopener ugc nofollow" target="_blank">Keyfactor Technical Team</a> published a blog post in 2016, <a class="af nu" href="https://www.keyfactor.com/blog/hidden-dangers-certificate-subject-alternative-names-sans/" rel="noopener ugc nofollow" target="_blank">Hidden Dangers: Certificate Subject Alternative Names (SANs)</a>, which describes the dangerous configuration that enables the domain escalation technique <a class="af nu" href="https://twitter.com/harmj0y" rel="noopener ugc nofollow" target="_blank">Will Schroeder</a> and<a class="af nu" href="https://twitter.com/tifkin_" rel="noopener ugc nofollow" target="_blank"> Lee Chagolla-Christensen</a> later named “ESC6” in their ADCS whitepaper<a class="af nu" href="https://specterops.io/wp-content/uploads/sites/3/2022/06/Certified_Pre-Owned.pdf" rel="noopener ugc nofollow" target="_blank"> Certified Pre-Owned</a>. <a class="af nu" href="https://x.com/ly4k_" rel="noopener ugc nofollow" target="_blank">Oliver Lyak</a> found and described ESC9 and ESC10 in the blog post <a class="af nu" href="https://research.ifcr.dk/certipy-4-0-esc9-esc10-bloodhound-gui-new-authentication-and-request-methods-and-more-7237d88061f7" rel="noopener ugc nofollow" target="_blank">Certipy 4.0: ESC9 &amp; ESC10, BloodHound GUI, New Authentication and Request Methods — and more!</a>. Much kudos to these people for sharing their research with the community.</p><p id="4960" class="pw-post-body-paragraph mw mx gu my b mz na nb nc nd ne nf ng nh ni nj nk nl nm nn no np nq nr ns nt gn bk">The configuration of <em class="nv">implicit certificate mapping </em>is a common factor impacting the three techniques, and it is the first topic we will explore.</p><h1 id="8e38" class="nw nx gu bf ny nz oa ob oc od oe of og oh oi oj ok ol om on oo op oq or os ot bk">Certificate Mapping</h1><p id="e775" class="pw-post-body-paragraph mw mx gu my b mz ou nb nc nd ov nf ng nh ow nj nk nl ox nn no np oy nr ns nt gn bk">When you use a password to authenticate in Active Directory (AD), you must specify both a username and a password. A domain controller (DC) will look up the AD account with a matching username and verify that the password you provided is correct for this account.</p><p id="683e" class="pw-post-body-paragraph mw mx gu my b mz na nb nc nd ne nf ng nh ni nj nk nl nm nn no np nq nr ns nt gn bk">When you use a certificate instead of a password to authenticate, the DC performs “<em class="nv">certificate mapping</em>” to verify that the certificate “maps” to the AD account you specified.</p><h2 id="d8fd" class="oz nx gu bf ny pa pb dy oc pc pd ea og nh pe pf pg nl ph pi pj np pk pl pm pn bk">Implicit Certificate Mapping</h2><p id="659f" class="pw-post-body-paragraph mw mx gu my b mz ou nb nc nd ov nf ng nh ow nj nk nl ox nn no np oy nr ns nt gn bk">When AD user Alice enrolls a certificate from ADCS, the Certificate Authority (CA) includes the <code class="cx po pp pq pr b">userPrincipalName</code> (UPN) of her account in the issued certificate Subject Alternative Name (SAN). When Alice attempts to authenticate with her username and the certificate, the DC finds Alice’s AD account with the username and verifies that the certificate maps to the account by confirming the certificate SAN UPN matches the AD account’s UPN.</p><p id="d908" class="pw-post-body-paragraph mw mx gu my b mz na nb nc nd ne nf ng nh ni nj nk nl nm nn no np nq nr ns nt gn bk">Not all users have a UPN. To account for that, the DC attempts to map the SAN UPN without the “@domain.name” part to the <code class="cx po pp pq pr b">sAMAccountName</code> of the AD account as a second try. As a last try, the DC repeats the second attempt but with a “$” added, which gives a match if Alice is a computer since the <code class="cx po pp pq pr b">sAMAccountName</code> of computers end with “$”.</p><p id="feec" class="pw-post-body-paragraph mw mx gu my b mz na nb nc nd ne nf ng nh ni nj nk nl nm nn no np nq nr ns nt gn bk">The DC will check if there are any other accounts with a matching attribute for the given SAN value before moving to the second and third try. If that is the case, then the authentication attempt will fail.</p><p id="29f6" class="pw-post-body-paragraph mw mx gu my b mz na nb nc nd ne nf ng nh ni nj nk nl nm nn no np nq nr ns nt gn bk">This concept is known as <em class="nv">UPN mapping</em>. There is a similar concept known as <em class="nv">DNS mapping</em>, where the <code class="cx po pp pq pr b">dnsHostName</code> (DNS) attribute is used instead. In that case, the DC will attempt mapping the hostname part of the SAN DNS value to the <code class="cx po pp pq pr b">sAMAccountName</code>.</p><h2 id="43e8" class="oz nx gu bf ny pa pb dy oc pc pd ea og nh pe pf pg nl ph pi pj np pk pl pm pn bk">Abusing Implicit Certificate Mapping</h2><p id="ea39" class="pw-post-body-paragraph mw mx gu my b mz ou nb nc nd ov nf ng nh ow nj nk nl ox nn no np oy nr ns nt gn bk">Oliver Lyak found that he could abuse weaknesses in the implicit mapping logic to enroll a certificate as one account and authenticate as another. You can read about Oliver’s cool finding here: <a class="af nu" href="https://research.ifcr.dk/certifried-active-directory-domain-privilege-escalation-cve-2022-26923-9e098fe298f4" rel="noopener ugc nofollow" target="_blank">Certifried: Active Directory Domain Privilege Escalation (CVE-2022–26923)</a>.</p><p id="6e42" class="pw-post-body-paragraph mw mx gu my b mz na nb nc nd ne nf ng nh ni nj nk nl nm nn no np nq nr ns nt gn bk">TLDR: You can change an AD victim account’s UPN or DNS attribute to match the <code class="cx po pp pq pr b">sAMAccountName</code> of any target account, such that when you enroll a certificate as the victim, the certificate will contain the manipulated attribute value which maps to the target account. You can then use this certificate to login as the target account.</p><p id="7c4a" class="pw-post-body-paragraph mw mx gu my b mz na nb nc nd ne nf ng nh ni nj nk nl nm nn no np nq nr ns nt gn bk">The overall steps of the attack are:</p><ol class=""><li id="306e" class="mw mx gu my b mz na nb nc nd ne nf ng nh ni nj nk nl nm nn no np nq nr ns nt ps pt pu bk">Modify a victim account’s UPN/DNS attribute to match a target’s <code class="cx po pp pq pr b">sAMAccountName</code></li><li id="75b1" class="mw mx gu my b mz pv nb nc nd pw nf ng nh px nj nk nl py nn no np pz nr ns nt ps pt pu bk">Enroll a certificate as the victim</li><li id="b4c7" class="mw mx gu my b mz pv nb nc nd pw nf ng nh px nj nk nl py nn no np pz nr ns nt ps pt pu bk">Authenticate as the target account using the certificate</li></ol><p id="4e36" class="pw-post-body-paragraph mw mx gu my b mz na nb nc nd ne nf ng nh ni nj nk nl nm nn no np nq nr ns nt gn bk">If you are modifying the DNS attribute in step 1, then you first remove any <code class="cx po pp pq pr b">servicePrincipalNames</code> (SPNs) that contains the victim account’s DNS name. The DC will automatically attempt to update those SPNs if not deleted, which causes a conflict with the target’s SPN and the DNS change failing.</p><p id="197b" class="pw-post-body-paragraph mw mx gu my b mz na nb nc nd ne nf ng nh ni nj nk nl nm nn no np nq nr ns nt gn bk">If you are modifying the UPN attribute in step 1, then you have to change the victim’s UPN once again after step 2 to avoid the certificate mapping to the victim.</p><p id="e4d3" class="pw-post-body-paragraph mw mx gu my b mz na nb nc nd ne nf ng nh ni nj nk nl nm nn no np nq nr ns nt gn bk">Here is an animation example of how the abuse goes with Kerberos UPN mapping:</p><figure class="qa qb qc qd qe qf"><div class="qg jf l fj"><div class="qh qi l"></div></div></figure><h2 id="8f5c" class="oz nx gu bf ny pa pb dy oc pc pd ea og nh pe pf pg nl ph pi pj np pk pl pm pn bk">The Patch</h2><p id="501c" class="pw-post-body-paragraph mw mx gu my b mz ou nb nc nd ov nf ng nh ow nj nk nl ox nn no np oy nr ns nt gn bk">Microsoft patched the vulnerability by making enterprise CAs add a new certificate extension (i.e., <code class="cx po pp pq pr b">szOID_NTDS_CA_SECURITY_EXT</code>) to new certificates containing the enrollee’s SID. The extension is commonly referred to as the SID (or security) extension. The extension enables the DC to verify that the account that enrolled the certificate is also the account the certificate maps to and otherwise disallow the authentication attempt. Microsoft refers to the use of the SID extension as <em class="nv">strong</em> mapping.</p><p id="73e6" class="pw-post-body-paragraph mw mx gu my b mz na nb nc nd ne nf ng nh ni nj nk nl nm nn no np nq nr ns nt gn bk">Microsoft could not enforce strong mapping overnight as all existing certificates would not have this new SID extension. They therefore introduced a new registry value named <code class="cx po pp pq pr b">StrongCertificateBindingEnforcement</code> under <code class="cx po pp pq pr b">HKLM\System\CurrentControlSet\Services\Kdc</code> on DCs to roll out the strong mapping enforcement for Kerberos in a controlled manner. Strong certificate mapping for Kerberos has the following modes:</p><figure class="qa qb qc qd qe qf qj qk paragraph-image"><div role="button" tabindex="0" class="qm qn fj qo bh qp"><div class="qj qk ql"><picture><source srcSet="https://miro.medium.com/v2/resize:fit:640/format:webp/1*G2NJnKMCKo7lBHVlHwlg4g.png 640w, https://miro.medium.com/v2/resize:fit:720/format:webp/1*G2NJnKMCKo7lBHVlHwlg4g.png (-webkit-min-device-pixel-ratio: 2.5) and (max-width: 700px) 80vw, (min-resolution: 2dppx) and (max-width: 700px) 100vw, (-webkit-min-device-pixel-ratio: 2) and (max-width: 700px) 100vw, 700px"/><img alt="" class="bh md qq c" width="700" height="298" loading="lazy" role="presentation"/></picture></div></div></figure><p id="7b2a" class="pw-post-body-paragraph mw mx gu my b mz na nb nc nd ne nf ng nh ni nj nk nl nm nn no np nq nr ns nt gn bk">The two weak modes are only temporarily available. The disabled mode is already unsupported, meaning that if you configure a DC with this mode, it behaves as in compatibility mode. Full enforcement is currently scheduled for “<em class="nv">Feb 11, 2025, or later”</em>. This date has been postponed three times, so it may change again.</p><p id="f6a8" class="pw-post-body-paragraph mw mx gu my b mz na nb nc nd ne nf ng nh ni nj nk nl nm nn no np nq nr ns nt gn bk">Strong mapping works with features that enable legitimate impersonation like <em class="nv">Enrollee Supplies Subject</em> (ESC1) or <em class="nv">Enroll On Behalf Of</em> (ESC3). The CA allows the enrollee to specify the SID extension in case the certificate template has the Enrollee Supplies Subject flag and it will add the SID extension by itself when you send an Enroll On Behalf Of request.</p><p id="7cc2" class="pw-post-body-paragraph mw mx gu my b mz na nb nc nd ne nf ng nh ni nj nk nl nm nn no np nq nr ns nt gn bk">Microsoft also introduced a new certificate template flag called <code class="cx po pp pq pr b">NO_SECURITY_EXTENSION</code>. If enabled, the enterprise CA avoids adding the SID extension to certificates of the given template. This enables admins to keep using applications/scripts that do not add or support the new SID extension. It is also interesting from an abuse perspective, as it enables us to obtain a new certificate that we can use for weak implicit mapping. The flag is disabled by default, though.</p><p id="57f9" class="pw-post-body-paragraph mw mx gu my b mz na nb nc nd ne nf ng nh ni nj nk nl nm nn no np nq nr ns nt gn bk"><strong class="my gv">Schannel</strong></p><p id="73c5" class="pw-post-body-paragraph mw mx gu my b mz na nb nc nd ne nf ng nh ni nj nk nl nm nn no np nq nr ns nt gn bk">The patch also updated how Schannel mapping works, disabling weak mapping by default:</p><figure class="qa qb qc qd qe qf qj qk paragraph-image"><div role="button" tabindex="0" class="qm qn fj qo bh qp"><div class="qj qk qr"><picture><source srcSet="https://miro.medium.com/v2/resize:fit:640/format:webp/1*GnD5XlvA0TZxEuTVTe0Elg.png 640w, https://miro.medium.com/v2/resize:fit:720/format:webp/1*GnD5XlvA0TZxEuTVTe0Elg.png 720w, https://miro.medium.com/v2/resize:fit:750/format:webp/1*GnD5XlvA0TZxEuTVTe0Elg.png 750w, https://miro.medium.com/v2/resize:fit:786/format:webp/1*GnD5XlvA0TZxEuTVTe0Elg.png 786w, https://miro.medium.com/v2/resize:fit:828/format:webp/1*GnD5XlvA0TZxEuTVTe0Elg.png 828w, https://miro.medium.com/v2/resize:fit:1100/format:webp/1*GnD5XlvA0TZxEuTVTe0Elg.png 1100w, https://miro.medium.com/v2/resize:fit:1400/format:webp/1*GnD5XlvA0TZxEuTVTe0Elg.png 1400w" sizes="(min-resolution: 4dppx) and (max-width: 700px) 50vw, (-webkit-min-device-pixel-ratio: 4) and type="image/webp"/><source data-testid="og" srcSet="https://miro.medium.com/v2/resize:fit:640/1*GnD5XlvA0TZxEuTVTe0Elg.png 640w, https://miro.medium.com/v2/resize:fit:720/1*GnD5XlvA0TZxEuTVTe0Elg.png 720w, https://miro.medium.com/v2/resize:fit:750/1*GnD5XlvA0TZxEuTVTe0Elg.png 750w, https://miro.medium.com/v2/resize:fit:786/1*GnD5XlvA0TZxEuTVTe0Elg.png 786w, https://miro.medium.com/v2/resize:fit:828/1*GnD5XlvA0TZxEuTVTe0Elg.png 828w, https://miro.medium.com/v2/resize:fit:1100/1*GnD5XlvA0TZxEuTVTe0Elg.png 1100w, 50vw, (min-resolution: 3dppx) and (max-width: 700px) 67vw, (-webkit-min-device-pixel-ratio: 3) and (max-width: 700px) 65vw, (min-resolution: 2.5dppx) and (max-width: 700px) 80vw, (-webkit-min-device-pixel-ratio: 2.5) and (max-width: 700px) 80vw, (min-resolution: 2dppx) and (max-width: 700px) 100vw, (-webkit-min-device-pixel-ratio: 2) and (max-width: 700px) 100vw, 700px"/><img alt="" class="bh md qq c" width="700" height="447" loading="lazy" role="presentation"/></picture></div></div></figure><p id="0573" class="pw-post-body-paragraph mw mx gu my b mz na nb nc nd ne nf ng nh ni nj nk nl nm nn no np nq nr ns nt gn bk">DCs store these flags in the registry value named <code class="cx po pp pq pr b">CertificateMappingMethods</code> under <code class="cx po pp pq pr b">HKLM\System\CurrentControlSet\Control\SecurityProviders\Schannel</code>.</p><p id="0958" class="pw-post-body-paragraph mw mx gu my b mz na nb nc nd ne nf ng nh ni nj nk nl nm nn no np nq nr ns nt gn bk">Schannel tries the Kerberos Service-For-User-To-Self (S4U2Self) mappings first. The 0x08 S4U2Self mapping means the DC uses the Kerberos mapping logic. I do not know what the S4U2Self explicit mapping is for or what it does. You would think it is for Kerberos explicit mapping, but that seems to be enabled by 0x08 S4U2Self based on the tests I have done.</p><p id="f102" class="pw-post-body-paragraph mw mx gu my b mz na nb nc nd ne nf ng nh ni nj nk nl nm nn no np nq nr ns nt gn bk">The DC continues to 0x04 UPN mapping if it is enabled if S4U2Self fails. It is this flag that enables weak implicit mapping over Schannel — both UPN mapping and DNS mapping. This mapping does not support the SID extension and ignores it if present.</p><h1 id="a19b" class="nw nx gu bf ny nz oa ob oc od oe of og oh oi oj ok ol om on oo op oq or os ot bk">ESC9 and ESC10 — Abuse of Weak Certificate Mapping</h1><p id="eb0d" class="pw-post-body-paragraph mw mx gu my b mz ou nb nc nd ov nf ng nh ow nj nk nl ox nn no np oy nr ns nt gn bk">Oliver found that the patch did not prevent his implicit certificate mapping abuse entirely, and it was still possible under two cases, which he named ESC9 and ESC10:</p><p id="a465" class="pw-post-body-paragraph mw mx gu my b mz na nb nc nd ne nf ng nh ni nj nk nl nm nn no np nq nr ns nt gn bk">ESC9 requirements (with <code class="cx po pp pq pr b">NO_SECURITY_EXTENSION</code> template):</p><ul class=""><li id="0af2" class="mw mx gu my b mz na nb nc nd ne nf ng nh ni nj nk nl nm nn no np nq nr ns nt qs pt pu bk">Certificate template has the <code class="cx po pp pq pr b">NO_SECURITY_EXTENSION</code> flag enabled</li><li id="703d" class="mw mx gu my b mz pv nb nc nd pw nf ng nh px nj nk nl py nn no np pz nr ns nt qs pt pu bk"><code class="cx po pp pq pr b">StrongCertificateBindingEnforcement</code> set to 0/1 (Disabled/Compatibility) OR <code class="cx po pp pq pr b">CertificateMappingMethods</code> contains UPN flag</li></ul><p id="7c4d" class="pw-post-body-paragraph mw mx gu my b mz na nb nc nd ne nf ng nh ni nj nk nl nm nn no np nq nr ns nt gn bk">ESC10 requirements (without <code class="cx po pp pq pr b">NO_SECURITY_EXTENSION</code> template):</p><ul class=""><li id="aa50" class="mw mx gu my b mz na nb nc nd ne nf ng nh ni nj nk nl nm nn no np nq nr ns nt qs pt pu bk"><code class="cx po pp pq pr b">StrongCertificateBindingEnforcement</code> set to 0 (Disabled) OR <code class="cx po pp pq pr b">CertificateMappingMethods</code> contains UPN flag</li></ul><p id="eb7a" class="pw-post-body-paragraph mw mx gu my b mz na nb nc nd ne nf ng nh ni nj nk nl nm nn no np nq nr ns nt gn bk">The <em class="nv">Abusing Implicit Certificate Mapping</em> section explains the concept and steps of these techniques. You can also check out Oliver’s blog post <a class="af nu" href="https://research.ifcr.dk/certipy-4-0-esc9-esc10-bloodhound-gui-new-authentication-and-request-methods-and-more-7237d88061f7" rel="noopener ugc nofollow" target="_blank">Certipy 4.0: ESC9 &amp; ESC10, BloodHound GUI, New Authentication and Request Methods — and more!</a> for examples of the techniques executed in a lab environment.</p><h2 id="9f0e" class="oz nx gu bf ny pa pb dy oc pc pd ea og nh pe pf pg nl ph pi pj np pk pl pm pn bk">ESC9 and ESC10 Requirements in BloodHound</h2><p id="e949" class="pw-post-body-paragraph mw mx gu my b mz ou nb nc nd ov nf ng nh ow nj nk nl ox nn no np oy nr ns nt gn bk">It has been a couple of years since Oliver published ESC9 and ESC10 and the Kerberos strong mapping disabled mode is now unsupported. We can, therefore, eliminate that as an enabling factor for the two techniques. That leaves us with the following cases:</p><figure class="qa qb qc qd qe qf qj qk paragraph-image"><div role="button" tabindex="0" class="qm qn fj qo bh qp"><div class="qj qk qt"><picture><source alt="" class="bh md qq c" width="700" height="265" loading="lazy" role="presentation"/></picture></div></div></figure><p id="a88a" class="pw-post-body-paragraph mw mx gu my b mz na nb nc nd ne nf ng nh ni nj nk nl nm nn no np nq nr ns nt gn bk">The SID extension does not affect Schannel UPN mapping, so we can remove the second ESC9 case. As a consequence, ESC9 in BloodHound is for Kerberos authentication and ESC10 for Schannel.</p><p id="26f4" class="pw-post-body-paragraph mw mx gu my b mz na nb nc nd ne nf ng nh ni nj nk nl nm nn no np nq nr ns nt gn bk">We have split the techniques up into an A and B scenario in BloodHound based on what type of implicit mapping they abuse:</p><figure class="qa qb qc qd qe qf qj qk paragraph-image"><div role="button" tabindex="0" class="qm qn fj qo bh qp"><div class="qj qk qu"><picture><source srcSet="https://miro.medium.com/v2/resize:fit:640/format:webp/1*YH0QcXXpx-CXj6tYJpMU8A.png 640w, https://miro.medium.com/v2/resize:fit:720/format:webp/1*YH0QcXXpx-CXj6tYJpMU8A.png 720w, type="image/webp"/><source data-testid="og" srcSet="https://miro.medium.com/v2/resize:fit:640/1*YH0QcXXpx-CXj6tYJpMU8A.png 640w, https://miro.medium.com/v2/resize:fit:720/1*YH0QcXXpx-CXj6tYJpMU8A.png (-webkit-min-device-pixel-ratio: 2.5) and (max-width: 700px) 80vw, (min-resolution: 2dppx) and (max-width: 700px) 100vw, (-webkit-min-device-pixel-ratio: 2) and (max-width: 700px) 100vw, 700px"/><img alt="" class="bh md qq c" width="700" height="307" loading="lazy" role="presentation"/></picture></div></div></figure><p id="d4ae" class="pw-post-body-paragraph mw mx gu my b mz na nb nc nd ne nf ng nh ni nj nk nl nm nn no np nq nr ns nt gn bk">This split allows us to provide more precise information and instructions in the edge entity panels, as there are specific details you need to be aware of for both UPN and DNS mapping abuse.</p><p id="750f" class="pw-post-body-paragraph mw mx gu my b mz na nb nc nd ne nf ng nh ni nj nk nl nm nn no np nq nr ns nt gn bk">All four technique scenarios have the following requirements:</p><p id="2c8e" class="pw-post-body-paragraph mw mx gu my b mz na nb nc nd ne nf ng nh ni nj nk nl nm nn no np nq nr ns nt gn bk">1) The principal (i.e., attacker) has control over a victim account<br/>2) The victim has enrollment rights on a certificate template<br/>3) The certificate template has manager approval disabled<br/>4) The certificate template does not require any authorized signatures<br/>5) The certificate template is published to an enterprise CA<br/>6) The victim has enrollment rights on the enterprise CA<br/>7) The enterprise CA is trusted for NT authentication<br/>8) The enterprise CA’s certificate chain is trusted</p><p id="fb90" class="pw-post-body-paragraph mw mx gu my b mz na nb nc nd ne nf ng nh ni nj nk nl nm nn no np nq nr ns nt gn bk">ESC9 has the additional requirements for Kerberos authentication to pass:</p><p id="f3fb" class="pw-post-body-paragraph mw mx gu my b mz na nb nc nd ne nf ng nh ni nj nk nl nm nn no np nq nr ns nt gn bk">9) The certificate template defines EKUs that enable Kerberos authentication<br/>10) The certificate template has the <code class="cx po pp pq pr b">NO_SECURITY_EXTENSION</code> flag<br/>11) A DC in the forest supports weak Kerberos certificate mapping</p><p id="e41f" class="pw-post-body-paragraph mw mx gu my b mz na nb nc nd ne nf ng nh ni nj nk nl nm nn no np nq nr ns nt gn bk">ESC10 has similarly these additional requirement for Schannel authentication:</p><p id="b672" class="pw-post-body-paragraph mw mx gu my b mz na nb nc nd ne nf ng nh ni nj nk nl nm nn no np nq nr ns nt gn bk">12) The certificate template defines EKUs that enable Schannel authentication<br/>13) A DC in the forest has Schannel UPN mapping enabled</p><p id="b44c" class="pw-post-body-paragraph mw mx gu my b mz na nb nc nd ne nf ng nh ni nj nk nl nm nn no np nq nr ns nt gn bk">Additionally, for the A scenarios, UPN mapping must work, which requires:</p><p id="9236" class="pw-post-body-paragraph mw mx gu my b mz na nb nc nd ne nf ng nh ni nj nk nl nm nn no np nq nr ns nt gn bk">14) The certificate template requires the enrollee’s UPN in SAN<br/>15) If the victim is a user, then the certificate template must NOT require the enrollee’s DNS in SAN</p><p id="573a" class="pw-post-body-paragraph mw mx gu my b mz na nb nc nd ne nf ng nh ni nj nk nl nm nn no np nq nr ns nt gn bk">For the B scenarios, DNS mapping must work, which requires:</p><p id="a8a2" class="pw-post-body-paragraph mw mx gu my b mz na nb nc nd ne nf ng nh ni nj nk nl nm nn no np nq nr ns nt gn bk">16) The certificate template requires the enrollee’s DNS in SAN<br/>17) The victim account is a computer</p><p id="430c" class="pw-post-body-paragraph mw mx gu my b mz na nb nc nd ne nf ng nh ni nj nk nl nm nn no np nq nr ns nt gn bk">The <a class="af nu" rel="noopener ugc nofollow" target="_blank" href="/adcs-attack-paths-in-bloodhound-part-1-799f3d3b03cf">Part 1</a> blog post describes requirements 2–9 in detail and how we have implemented the checks for those in BloodHound. We will go through how we have implemented the remaining requirements.</p><p id="f440" class="pw-post-body-paragraph mw mx gu my b mz na nb nc nd ne nf ng nh ni nj nk nl nm nn no np nq nr ns nt gn bk"><strong class="my gv">1) The principal (i.e., attacker) has control over a victim account</strong></p><p id="6a51" class="pw-post-body-paragraph mw mx gu my b mz na nb nc nd ne nf ng nh ni nj nk nl nm nn no np nq nr ns nt gn bk">As the attacker, we need to have sufficient control over the victim account in order to:</p><ol class=""><li id="37b3" class="mw mx gu my b mz na nb nc nd ne nf ng nh ni nj nk nl nm nn no np nq nr ns nt ps pt pu bk">Modify the UPN/DNS attribute of the victim</li><li id="6851" class="mw mx gu my b mz pv nb nc nd pw nf ng nh px nj nk nl py nn no np pz nr ns nt ps pt pu bk">Impersonate the victim (to request a certificate)</li></ol><p id="df01" class="pw-post-body-paragraph mw mx gu my b mz na nb nc nd ne nf ng nh ni nj nk nl nm nn no np nq nr ns nt gn bk">There are many combinations of permissions that provide sufficient control and you can do the impersonation many different ways. You can, for example, impersonate a victim by abusing a ForceChangePassword edge to perform a password reset attack; alternatively, you can abuse an AdminTo edge to request a certificate as a computer victim from the host. However, those edges will not provide sufficient permissions to change the UPN/DNS attributes. We therefore check for the edges in BloodHound that give the permissions to do both the attribute manipulation and impersonation:</p><ul class=""><li id="94ea" class="mw mx gu my b mz na nb nc nd ne nf ng nh ni nj nk nl nm nn no np nq nr ns nt qs pt pu bk">GenericAll</li><li id="ecf0" class="mw mx gu my b mz pv nb nc nd pw nf ng nh px nj nk nl py nn no np pz nr ns nt qs pt pu bk">GenericWrite</li><li id="d22d" class="mw mx gu my b mz pv nb nc nd pw nf ng nh px nj nk nl py nn no np pz nr ns nt qs pt pu bk">WriteDacl</li><li id="5339" class="mw mx gu my b mz pv nb nc nd pw nf ng nh px nj nk nl py nn no np pz nr ns nt qs pt pu bk">WriteOwner</li><li id="935e" class="mw mx gu my b mz pv nb nc nd pw nf ng nh px nj nk nl py nn no np pz nr ns nt qs pt pu bk">Owns</li></ul><p id="cad7" class="pw-post-body-paragraph mw mx gu my b mz na nb nc nd ne nf ng nh ni nj nk nl nm nn no np nq nr ns nt gn bk"><strong class="my gv">10) The certificate template has the </strong><code class="cx po pp pq pr b"><strong class="my gv">NO_SECURITY_EXTENSION</strong></code><strong class="my gv"> flag</strong></p><p id="5549" class="pw-post-body-paragraph mw mx gu my b mz na nb nc nd ne nf ng nh ni nj nk nl nm nn no np nq nr ns nt gn bk">The certificate template attribute <code class="cx po pp pq pr b">msPKI-Enrollment-Flag</code> holds the (<code class="cx po pp pq pr b">CT_FLAG_</code>)<code class="cx po pp pq pr b">NO_SECURITY_EXTENSION</code> flag. In BloodHound, we check if templates have the flag enabled and store it as a boolean property on the CertTemplate node named <em class="nv">No Security Extension</em> (<code class="cx po pp pq pr b">nosecurityextension</code>).</p><p id="5cff" class="pw-post-body-paragraph mw mx gu my b mz na nb nc nd ne nf ng nh ni nj nk nl nm nn no np nq nr ns nt gn bk"><strong class="my gv">11) A DC in the forest supports weak Kerberos certificate mapping</strong></p><p id="4366" class="pw-post-body-paragraph mw mx gu my b mz na nb nc nd ne nf ng nh ni nj nk nl nm nn no np nq nr ns nt gn bk">The DC supports weak Kerberos certificate mapping if it is in compatibility mode (i.e., the registry key value <code class="cx po pp pq pr b">StrongCertificateBindingEnforcement</code> is 0 or 1). It is in compatibility mode by default for now, but that might change next year as described earlier.</p><p id="53e9" class="pw-post-body-paragraph mw mx gu my b mz na nb nc nd ne nf ng nh ni nj nk nl nm nn no np nq nr ns nt gn bk">The computer node in BloodHound has been updated with the following properties that reveal the Kerberos strong certificate mapping configuration:</p><ul class=""><li id="6934" class="mw mx gu my b mz na nb nc nd ne nf ng nh ni nj nk nl nm nn no np nq nr ns nt qs pt pu bk"><em class="nv">Strong Certificate Binding Enforcement (Raw)</em> (<code class="cx po pp pq pr b">strongcertificatebindingenforcementraw</code>): The raw integer value of the registry setting</li><li id="8922" class="mw mx gu my b mz pv nb nc nd pw nf ng nh px nj nk nl py nn no np pz nr ns nt qs pt pu bk"><em class="nv">Strong Certificate Binding Enforcement </em>(<code class="cx po pp pq pr b">strongcertificatebindingenforcement</code>): A string explaining the meaning of the raw value: <em class="nv">Disabled</em> (0), <em class="nv">Compatibility mode</em> (1), or <em class="nv">Full enforcement mode </em>(2)</li></ul><p id="5649" class="pw-post-body-paragraph mw mx gu my b mz na nb nc nd ne nf ng nh ni nj nk nl nm nn no np nq nr ns nt gn bk">It only requires a single DC in the forest with compatibility mode, as the attacker can target an account of that given domain and authenticate against this specific DC. BloodHound will verify that at least one such DC exists.</p><p id="9ae9" class="pw-post-body-paragraph mw mx gu my b mz na nb nc nd ne nf ng nh ni nj nk nl nm nn no np nq nr ns nt gn bk"><strong class="my gv">12) The certificate template defines EKUs that enable Schannel authentication</strong></p><p id="8aee" class="pw-post-body-paragraph mw mx gu my b mz na nb nc nd ne nf ng nh ni nj nk nl nm nn no np nq nr ns nt gn bk">Not all EKUs that enable Kerberos authentication works for Schannel authentication:</p><figure class="qa qb qc qd qe qf qj qk paragraph-image"><div role="button" tabindex="0" class="qm qn fj qo bh qp"><div class="qj qk qv"><picture><source srcSet="https://miro.medium.com/v2/resize:fit:640/format:webp/1*iysrU3FuHvfnpfeClq_CgA.png 640w, alt="" class="bh md qq c" width="700" height="476" loading="lazy" role="presentation"/></picture></div></div></figure><p id="3756" class="pw-post-body-paragraph mw mx gu my b mz na nb nc nd ne nf ng nh ni nj nk nl nm nn no np nq nr ns nt gn bk">To make life simple for BloodHound users, we have added a certificate template property named <em class="nv">Schannel Authentication Enabled</em> (<code class="cx po pp pq pr b">schannelauthenticationenabled</code>) which reveals if you can use certificates of a given template for Schannel authentication. The logic for creating the property besides the EKUs part is the same as for the <em class="nv">Authentication Enabled</em> (Kerberos) property described in <a class="af nu" rel="noopener ugc nofollow" target="_blank" href="/adcs-attack-paths-in-bloodhound-part-1-799f3d3b03cf">Part 1</a>.</p><p id="f29a" class="pw-post-body-paragraph mw mx gu my b mz na nb nc nd ne nf ng nh ni nj nk nl nm nn no np nq nr ns nt gn bk"><strong class="my gv">13) A DC in the forest has Schannel UPN mapping enabled</strong></p><p id="899e" class="pw-post-body-paragraph mw mx gu my b mz na nb nc nd ne nf ng nh ni nj nk nl nm nn no np nq nr ns nt gn bk">The DC registry key value <code class="cx po pp pq pr b">CertificateMappingMethods</code> must contain the 0x04 UPN flag to enable Schannel UPN/DNS mapping. This flag is not set by default.</p><p id="d560" class="pw-post-body-paragraph mw mx gu my b mz na nb nc nd ne nf ng nh ni nj nk nl nm nn no np nq nr ns nt gn bk">The computer node in BloodHound has been updated with the following properties that reveal the Schannel certificate mapping configuration:</p><ul class=""><li id="6624" class="mw mx gu my b mz na nb nc nd ne nf ng nh ni nj nk nl nm nn no np nq nr ns nt qs pt pu bk"><em class="nv">Certificate Mapping Methods (Raw)</em> (<code class="cx po pp pq pr b">certificatemappingmethodsraw</code>): The raw integer value of the registry setting</li><li id="d16d" class="mw mx gu my b mz pv nb nc nd pw nf ng nh px nj nk nl py nn no np pz nr ns nt qs pt pu bk"><em class="nv">Certificate Mapping Methods </em>(<code class="cx po pp pq pr b">certificatemappingmethods</code>): A list of the supported mappings based on the raw value:<br/>- <em class="nv">0x01: One-to-one (subject/issuer)<br/>- 0x02: Many-to-one (issuer certificate)<br/>- 0x04: User principal name (UPN/SAN)<br/>- 0x08: Kerberos service-for-user (S4U) certificate<br/>- 0x10: Kerberos service-for-user (S4U) explicit certificate</em></li></ul><p id="feee" class="pw-post-body-paragraph mw mx gu my b mz na nb nc nd ne nf ng nh ni nj nk nl nm nn no np nq nr ns nt gn bk">BloodHound will verify that at least one DC in the forest has the 0x04 flag in this setting.</p><p id="e6cc" class="pw-post-body-paragraph mw mx gu my b mz na nb nc nd ne nf ng nh ni nj nk nl nm nn no np nq nr ns nt gn bk"><strong class="my gv">14) The certificate template requires the enrollee’s UPN in SAN</strong></p><p id="d698" class="pw-post-body-paragraph mw mx gu my b mz na nb nc nd ne nf ng nh ni nj nk nl nm nn no np nq nr ns nt gn bk">The enterprise CA will put the enrollee’s UPN into the SAN of the issued certificate if the certificate template has one of the following flags in its <code class="cx po pp pq pr b">msPKI-Certificate-Name-Flag</code> attribute:</p><ul class=""><li id="485d" class="mw mx gu my b mz na nb nc nd ne nf ng nh ni nj nk nl nm nn no np nq nr ns nt qs pt pu bk"><code class="cx po pp pq pr b">CT_FLAG_SUBJECT_ALT_REQUIRE_UPN</code></li><li id="bd2a" class="mw mx gu my b mz pv nb nc nd pw nf ng nh px nj nk nl py nn no np pz nr ns nt qs pt pu bk"><code class="cx po pp pq pr b">CT_FLAG_SUBJECT_ALT_REQUIRE_SPN</code></li></ul><figure class="qa qb qc qd qe qf qj qk paragraph-image"><div role="button" tabindex="0" class="qm qn fj qo bh qp"><div class="qj qk qw"><picture><source srcSet="https://miro.medium.com/v2/resize:fit:640/format:webp/0*fVuNFQDMZHFT28CP 640w, https://miro.medium.com/v2/resize:fit:720/format:webp/0*fVuNFQDMZHFT28CP 720w, https://miro.medium.com/v2/resize:fit:750/format:webp/0*fVuNFQDMZHFT28CP 750w, https://miro.medium.com/v2/resize:fit:786/format:webp/0*fVuNFQDMZHFT28CP 786w, https://miro.medium.com/v2/resize:fit:828/format:webp/0*fVuNFQDMZHFT28CP 828w, https://miro.medium.com/v2/resize:fit:1100/format:webp/0*fVuNFQDMZHFT28CP 1100w, 50vw, (min-resolution: 3dppx) and (max-width: 700px) 67vw, (-webkit-min-device-pixel-ratio: 3) and (max-width: 700px) 65vw, (min-resolution: 2.5dppx) and (max-width: 700px) 80vw, (-webkit-min-device-pixel-ratio: 2.5) and (max-width: 700px) 80vw, (min-resolution: 2dppx) and (max-width: 700px) 100vw, (-webkit-min-device-pixel-ratio: 2) and (max-width: 700px) 100vw, 700px" type="image/webp"/><source data-testid="og" srcSet="https://miro.medium.com/v2/resize:fit:640/0*fVuNFQDMZHFT28CP 640w, https://miro.medium.com/v2/resize:fit:720/0*fVuNFQDMZHFT28CP 720w, https://miro.medium.com/v2/resize:fit:750/0*fVuNFQDMZHFT28CP 750w, https://miro.medium.com/v2/resize:fit:786/0*fVuNFQDMZHFT28CP 786w, https://miro.medium.com/v2/resize:fit:828/0*fVuNFQDMZHFT28CP 828w, https://miro.medium.com/v2/resize:fit:1100/0*fVuNFQDMZHFT28CP 1100w, https://miro.medium.com/v2/resize:fit:1400/0*fVuNFQDMZHFT28CP 1400w" sizes="(min-resolution: 4dppx) and (max-width: 700px) 50vw, (max-width: 700px) 100vw, 700px"/><img alt="" class="bh md qq c" width="700" height="377" loading="lazy" role="presentation"/></picture></div></div></figure><p id="71d7" class="pw-post-body-paragraph mw mx gu my b mz na nb nc nd ne nf ng nh ni nj nk nl nm nn no np nq nr ns nt gn bk">Yes — the SPN flag will make the UPN included. Microsoft has documented it <a class="af nu" href="https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/openspecs/windows_protocols/ms-wcce/a1f27ffb-7f74-4fa1-8841-7cde4ba0bcfe" rel="noopener ugc nofollow" target="_blank">here</a>.</p><p id="2b43" class="pw-post-body-paragraph mw mx gu my b mz na nb nc nd ne nf ng nh ni nj nk nl nm nn no np nq nr ns nt gn bk">We have implemented the two flags in BloodHound as boolean CertTemplate properties:</p><ul class=""><li id="8184" class="mw mx gu my b mz na nb nc nd ne nf ng nh ni nj nk nl nm nn no np nq nr ns nt qs pt pu bk"><em class="nv">Subject Alternative Name Require UPN</em> (<code class="cx po pp pq pr b">subjectaltrequireupn</code>)</li><li id="a03e" class="mw mx gu my b mz pv nb nc nd pw nf ng nh px nj nk nl py nn no np pz nr ns nt qs pt pu bk"><em class="nv">Subject Alternative Name Require SPN</em> (<code class="cx po pp pq pr b">subjectaltrequirespn</code>)</li></ul><p id="0985" class="pw-post-body-paragraph mw mx gu my b mz na nb nc nd ne nf ng nh ni nj nk nl nm nn no np nq nr ns nt gn bk">BloodHound verifies that the found CertTemplate has any of these properties set to true.</p><p id="04dd" class="pw-post-body-paragraph mw mx gu my b mz na nb nc nd ne nf ng nh ni nj nk nl nm nn no np nq nr ns nt gn bk"><strong class="my gv">15) If the victim is a user, then the certificate template must NOT require the enrollee’s DNS in SAN</strong></p><p id="f32c" class="pw-post-body-paragraph mw mx gu my b mz na nb nc nd ne nf ng nh ni nj nk nl nm nn no np nq nr ns nt gn bk">There are two certificate template <code class="cx po pp pq pr b">msPKI-Certificate-Name-Flag</code> flags that require the enrollee has their DNS attribute set:</p><ul class=""><li id="82d8" class="mw mx gu my b mz na nb nc nd ne nf ng nh ni nj nk nl nm nn no np nq nr ns nt qs pt pu bk"><code class="cx po pp pq pr b">CT_FLAG_SUBJECT_ALT_REQUIRE_DNS</code></li><li id="d3f6" class="mw mx gu my b mz pv nb nc nd pw nf ng nh px nj nk nl py nn no np pz nr ns nt qs pt pu bk"><code class="cx po pp pq pr b">CT_FLAG_SUBJECT_ALT_REQUIRE_DOMAIN_DNS</code></li></ul><p id="15e3" class="pw-post-body-paragraph mw mx gu my b mz na nb nc nd ne nf ng nh ni nj nk nl nm nn no np nq nr ns nt gn bk">The first one will include the DNS attribute in the SAN and the second one is just for the domain name of the attribute. If the enrollee does not have their DNS attribute set, enrollment will fail.</p><p id="9129" class="pw-post-body-paragraph mw mx gu my b mz na nb nc nd ne nf ng nh ni nj nk nl nm nn no np nq nr ns nt gn bk">The corresponding boolean CertTemplate properties are:</p><ul class=""><li id="df26" class="mw mx gu my b mz na nb nc nd ne nf ng nh ni nj nk nl nm nn no np nq nr ns nt qs pt pu bk"><em class="nv">Subject Alternative Name Require DNS</em> (<code class="cx po pp pq pr b">subjectaltrequiredns</code>)</li><li id="cd80" class="mw mx gu my b mz pv nb nc nd pw nf ng nh px nj nk nl py nn no np pz nr ns nt qs pt pu bk"><em class="nv">Subject Alternative Name Require Domain DNS</em> (<code class="cx po pp pq pr b">subjectaltrequiredomaindns</code>)</li></ul><p id="795a" class="pw-post-body-paragraph mw mx gu my b mz na nb nc nd ne nf ng nh ni nj nk nl nm nn no np nq nr ns nt gn bk">You cannot set the DNS attribute on a victim user account as the AD user class does not include the DNS attribute. BloodHound therefore verifies that both these flags are not set on the CertTemplate if the victim account is a user.</p><p id="4958" class="pw-post-body-paragraph mw mx gu my b mz na nb nc nd ne nf ng nh ni nj nk nl nm nn no np nq nr ns nt gn bk"><strong class="my gv">16) The certificate template requires the enrollee’s DNS in SAN</strong></p><p id="7667" class="pw-post-body-paragraph mw mx gu my b mz na nb nc nd ne nf ng nh ni nj nk nl nm nn no np nq nr ns nt gn bk">BloodHound verifies that the CertTemplate has the Subject Alternative Name Require DNS property described above set to true.</p><p id="0852" class="pw-post-body-paragraph mw mx gu my b mz na nb nc nd ne nf ng nh ni nj nk nl nm nn no np nq nr ns nt gn bk"><strong class="my gv">17) The victim account is a computer</strong></p><p id="46fa" class="pw-post-body-paragraph mw mx gu my b mz na nb nc nd ne nf ng nh ni nj nk nl nm nn no np nq nr ns nt gn bk">As we discussed above, users cannot enroll in certificate templates with the DNS flag. So when that is the case, the victim must be a computer node (or a gMSA or sMSA which is derived from the AD computer class).</p><h2 id="2a8f" class="oz nx gu bf ny pa pb dy oc pc pd ea og nh pe pf pg nl ph pi pj np pk pl pm pn bk">ESC9 and ESC10 — New BloodHound Edges</h2><p id="849b" class="pw-post-body-paragraph mw mx gu my b mz ou nb nc nd ov nf ng nh ow nj nk nl ox nn no np oy nr ns nt gn bk">If all the above requirements are met, then BloodHound creates the following new edges going from the attacking principal towards the root domain node:</p><ul class=""><li id="cb7a" class="mw mx gu my b mz na nb nc nd ne nf ng nh ni nj nk nl nm nn no np nq nr ns nt qs pt pu bk">ESC9 Scenario A: <a class="af nu" href="https://support.bloodhoundenterprise.io/hc/en-us/articles/22910551701019-ADCSESC9a" rel="noopener ugc nofollow" target="_blank">ADCSESC9a</a></li><li id="23a1" class="mw mx gu my b mz pv nb nc nd pw nf ng nh px nj nk nl py nn no np pz nr ns nt qs pt pu bk">ESC9 Scenario B: <a class="af nu" href="https://support.bloodhoundenterprise.io/hc/en-us/articles/22912477077787-ADCSESC9b" rel="noopener ugc nofollow" target="_blank">ADCSESC9b</a></li><li id="8d25" class="mw mx gu my b mz pv nb nc nd pw nf ng nh px nj nk nl py nn no np pz nr ns nt qs pt pu bk">ESC10 Scenario A: <a class="af nu" href="https://support.bloodhoundenterprise.io/hc/en-us/articles/22809172622107-ADCSESC10a" rel="noopener ugc nofollow" target="_blank">ADCSESC10a</a></li><li id="9d12" class="mw mx gu my b mz pv nb nc nd pw nf ng nh px nj nk nl py nn no np pz nr ns nt qs pt pu bk">ESC10 Scenario B: <a class="af nu" href="https://support.bloodhoundenterprise.io/hc/en-us/articles/22812770496667-ADCSESC10b" rel="noopener ugc nofollow" target="_blank">ADCSESC10b</a></li></ul><figure class="qa qb qc qd qe qf qj qk paragraph-image"><div role="button" tabindex="0" class="qm qn fj qo bh qp"><div class="qj qk qx"><picture><source srcSet="https://miro.medium.com/v2/resize:fit:640/format:webp/0*9_R6OdtkECRyQ_TH 640w, 1400w" sizes="(min-resolution: 4dppx) and (max-width: 700px) 50vw, (-webkit-min-device-pixel-ratio: 4) and (max-width: 700px) 50vw, (min-resolution: 3dppx) and (max-width: 700px) 67vw, role="presentation"/></picture></div></div></figure><p id="2193" class="pw-post-body-paragraph mw mx gu my b mz na nb nc nd ne nf ng nh ni nj nk nl nm nn no np nq nr ns nt gn bk">You can also find the edge information in the <a class="af nu" href="https://support.bloodhoundenterprise.io/hc/en-us/articles/22910551701019-ADCSESC9a" rel="noopener ugc nofollow" target="_blank">BloodHound support docs</a>.</p><p id="0d77" class="pw-post-body-paragraph mw mx gu my b mz na nb nc nd ne nf ng nh ni nj nk nl nm nn no np nq nr ns nt gn bk">Like the other ADCSESCx edges, the edges for ESC9 and ESC10 have a composition graph that BloodHound will show if you click on the <em class="nv">Composition</em> section in the entity panel, revealing the nodes that meet the requirements for the ADCSESCx edge. Here is the composition graph for the ADCSESC9a edge from Account Operators:</p><figure class="qa qb qc qd qe qf qj qk paragraph-image"><div role="button" tabindex="0" class="qm qn fj qo bh qp"><div class="qj qk qz"><picture><source srcSet="https://miro.medium.com/v2/resize:fit:640/format:webp/0*f8rVp21VMF4YTviF 640w, https://miro.medium.com/v2/resize:fit:720/format:webp/0*f8rVp21VMF4YTviF 720w, https://miro.medium.com/v2/resize:fit:750/format:webp/0*f8rVp21VMF4YTviF 750w, https://miro.medium.com/v2/resize:fit:786/format:webp/0*f8rVp21VMF4YTviF 786w, https://miro.medium.com/v2/resize:fit:828/format:webp/0*f8rVp21VMF4YTviF 828w, https://miro.medium.com/v2/resize:fit:1100/format:webp/0*f8rVp21VMF4YTviF 1100w, https://miro.medium.com/v2/resize:fit:1400/format:webp/0*f8rVp21VMF4YTviF 1400w" sizes="(min-resolution: 4dppx) and (max-width: 700px) 50vw, (-webkit-min-device-pixel-ratio: 4) and (max-width: 700px) 50vw, (min-resolution: 3dppx) and (max-width: 700px) 67vw, (-webkit-min-device-pixel-ratio: 3) and (max-width: 700px) 65vw, (min-resolution: 2.5dppx) and (max-width: 700px) 80vw, (-webkit-min-device-pixel-ratio: 2.5) and (max-width: 700px) 80vw, (min-resolution: 2dppx) and (max-width: 700px) 100vw, (-webkit-min-device-pixel-ratio: 2) and (max-width: 700px) 100vw, 700px" type="image/webp"/><source data-testid="og" srcSet="https://miro.medium.com/v2/resize:fit:640/0*f8rVp21VMF4YTviF 640w, https://miro.medium.com/v2/resize:fit:720/0*f8rVp21VMF4YTviF 720w, https://miro.medium.com/v2/resize:fit:750/0*f8rVp21VMF4YTviF 750w, https://miro.medium.com/v2/resize:fit:786/0*f8rVp21VMF4YTviF 786w, https://miro.medium.com/v2/resize:fit:828/0*f8rVp21VMF4YTviF 828w, https://miro.medium.com/v2/resize:fit:1100/0*f8rVp21VMF4YTviF 1100w, https://miro.medium.com/v2/resize:fit:1400/0*f8rVp21VMF4YTviF 1400w" sizes="(min-resolution: 4dppx) and (max-width: 700px) 50vw, (-webkit-min-device-pixel-ratio: 4) and (max-width: 700px) 50vw, (min-resolution: 3dppx) and (max-width: 700px) 67vw, (-webkit-min-device-pixel-ratio: 3) and (max-width: 700px) 65vw, (min-resolution: 2.5dppx) and (max-width: 700px) 80vw, (-webkit-min-device-pixel-ratio: 2.5) and (max-width: 700px) 80vw, (min-resolution: 2dppx) and (max-width: 700px) 100vw, (-webkit-min-device-pixel-ratio: 2) and (max-width: 700px) 100vw, 700px"/><img alt="" class="bh md qq c" width="700" height="1032" loading="lazy" role="presentation"/></picture></div></div></figure><p id="68e9" class="pw-post-body-paragraph mw mx gu my b mz na nb nc nd ne nf ng nh ni nj nk nl nm nn no np nq nr ns nt gn bk">Account Operators have control over two victim user accounts — that means we can abuse any of the two in an attack. The victims have enrollment rights on two certificate templates that meet the requirements for the attack. An enterprise CA has both templates published and grants enrollment rights to the victims through group membership. The enterprise CA chains up to a root CA for the domain which trusts the enterprise CA for NT authentication. At last, we have the computer node in the graph, which is the DC that supports weak Kerberos certificate mapping.</p><p id="5805" class="pw-post-body-paragraph mw mx gu my b mz na nb nc nd ne nf ng nh ni nj nk nl nm nn no np nq nr ns nt gn bk">The composition graph provides us with information about the components we need to execute the attack or remediate it. You can find the abuse steps for Windows and Linux in the abuse sections of the edge entity panel.</p><h1 id="5a94" class="nw nx gu bf ny nz oa ob oc od oe of og oh oi oj ok ol om on oo op oq or os ot bk">ESC6 — Abuse of <code class="cx po pp pq pr b">EDITF_ATTRIBUTESUBJECTALTNAME2</code></h1><p id="ee0a" class="pw-post-body-paragraph mw mx gu my b mz ou nb nc nd ov nf ng nh ow nj nk nl ox nn no np oy nr ns nt gn bk">The key requirement for ESC6 is that an enterprise CA has the dangerous <code class="cx po pp pq pr b">EDITF_ATTRIBUTESUBJECTALTNAME2</code> flag. When this flag is set, the enrollee may specify the SAN of the certificate in the certificate request using any certificate template. Therefore, an attacker can use any published certificate template they have enrollment rights on to obtain a certificate as any user or computer in the AD forest. Crazy stuff.</p><h2 id="2c54" class="oz nx gu bf ny pa pb dy oc pc pd ea og nh pe pf pg nl ph pi pj np pk pl pm pn bk">Strong Certificate Mapping and ESC6</h2><p id="2241" class="pw-post-body-paragraph mw mx gu my b mz ou nb nc nd ov nf ng nh ow nj nk nl ox nn no np oy nr ns nt gn bk">The MS patch discussed earlier makes the <code class="cx po pp pq pr b">EDITF_ATTRIBUTESUBJECTALTNAME2</code> functionality harder to use. The enterprise CA still allows you to specify the SAN, but it does not allow you to specify the SID extension.</p><p id="9e31" class="pw-post-body-paragraph mw mx gu my b mz na nb nc nd ne nf ng nh ni nj nk nl nm nn no np nq nr ns nt gn bk">However, some time after the patch, Microsoft released an update with an interesting feature called “<a class="af nu" href="https://techcommunity.microsoft.com/t5/ask-the-directory-services-team/preview-of-san-uri-for-certificate-strong-mapping-for-kb5014754/ba-p/3789785" rel="noopener ugc nofollow" target="_blank">SAN URI</a>”. This feature enables you to create a certificate valid for strong certificate mapping by including the principal SID in an URL component of the SAN instead of in the SID extension. The DC will first check the SID extension of the certificate and authentication will fail if there is a mismatch. But, if the certificate does not contain the SID extension, then the DC will check if the SAN URL contains a matching SID.</p><p id="39b1" class="pw-post-body-paragraph mw mx gu my b mz na nb nc nd ne nf ng nh ni nj nk nl nm nn no np nq nr ns nt gn bk">That means we can abuse the SAN URI feature together with a <code class="cx po pp pq pr b">NO_SECURITY_EXTENSION</code> flag certificate template to make the ESC6 attack work over Kerberos — even if the DC enforces strong certificate mapping.</p><figure class="qa qb qc qd qe qf qj qk paragraph-image"><div class="qj qk ra"><picture><source srcSet="https://miro.medium.com/v2/resize:fit:640/format:webp/0*S0BO6-tCqg_UpWIT 640w, https://miro.medium.com/v2/resize:fit:720/format:webp/0*S0BO6-tCqg_UpWIT 720w, https://miro.medium.com/v2/resize:fit:750/format:webp/0*S0BO6-tCqg_UpWIT 750w, https://miro.medium.com/v2/resize:fit:786/format:webp/0*S0BO6-tCqg_UpWIT 786w, https://miro.medium.com/v2/resize:fit:828/format:webp/0*S0BO6-tCqg_UpWIT 828w, https://miro.medium.com/v2/resize:fit:1100/format:webp/0*S0BO6-tCqg_UpWIT 1100w, https://miro.medium.com/v2/resize:fit:1000/format:webp/0*S0BO6-tCqg_UpWIT 1000w" sizes="(min-resolution: 4dppx) and (max-width: 700px) 50vw, (-webkit-min-device-pixel-ratio: 4) and (max-width: 700px) 50vw, (min-resolution: 3dppx) and (max-width: 700px) 67vw, (-webkit-min-device-pixel-ratio: 3) and (max-width: 700px) 65vw, (min-resolution: 2.5dppx) and (max-width: 700px) 80vw, (-webkit-min-device-pixel-ratio: 2.5) and (max-width: 700px) 80vw, (min-resolution: 2dppx) and (max-width: 700px) 100vw, (-webkit-min-device-pixel-ratio: 2) and (max-width: 700px) 100vw, 500px" type="image/webp"/><source data-testid="og" srcSet="https://miro.medium.com/v2/resize:fit:640/0*S0BO6-tCqg_UpWIT 640w, https://miro.medium.com/v2/resize:fit:720/0*S0BO6-tCqg_UpWIT 720w, https://miro.medium.com/v2/resize:fit:750/0*S0BO6-tCqg_UpWIT 750w, https://miro.medium.com/v2/resize:fit:786/0*S0BO6-tCqg_UpWIT 786w, https://miro.medium.com/v2/resize:fit:828/0*S0BO6-tCqg_UpWIT 828w, https://miro.medium.com/v2/resize:fit:1100/0*S0BO6-tCqg_UpWIT 1100w, https://miro.medium.com/v2/resize:fit:1000/0*S0BO6-tCqg_UpWIT 1000w" sizes="(min-resolution: 4dppx) and (max-width: 700px) 50vw, (-webkit-min-device-pixel-ratio: 4) and (max-width: 700px) 50vw, (min-resolution: 3dppx) and (max-width: 700px) 67vw, (-webkit-min-device-pixel-ratio: 3) and (max-width: 700px) 65vw, (min-resolution: 2.5dppx) and (max-width: 700px) 80vw, (-webkit-min-device-pixel-ratio: 2.5) and (max-width: 700px) 80vw, (min-resolution: 2dppx) and (max-width: 700px) 100vw, (-webkit-min-device-pixel-ratio: 2) and (max-width: 700px) 100vw, 500px"/><img alt="" class="bh md qq c" width="500" height="759" loading="lazy" role="presentation"/></picture></div></figure><p id="021c" class="pw-post-body-paragraph mw mx gu my b mz na nb nc nd ne nf ng nh ni nj nk nl nm nn no np nq nr ns nt gn bk">You can specify the SAN URL component when you enroll a certificate using Certify with the recently added <code class="cx po pp pq pr b">/url</code> parameter:</p><p id="83b9" class="pw-post-body-paragraph mw mx gu my b mz na nb nc nd ne nf ng nh ni nj nk nl nm nn no np nq nr ns nt gn bk"><code class="cx po pp pq pr b">Certify.exe request /ca:SERVER\ca-name /template:ESC6-Template /altname:target /url:tag:microsoft.com,2022–09–14:sid:S-1–5–21–2697957641–2271029196–387917394–2136</code></p><p id="0d82" class="pw-post-body-paragraph mw mx gu my b mz na nb nc nd ne nf ng nh ni nj nk nl nm nn no np nq nr ns nt gn bk">The value of the URL component must follow this pattern of the above example with the microsoft.com tag and the specific date.</p><p id="42d6" class="pw-post-body-paragraph mw mx gu my b mz na nb nc nd ne nf ng nh ni nj nk nl nm nn no np nq nr ns nt gn bk">For Schannel, ESC6 relies on the same UPN flag as ESC10.</p><h2 id="daeb" class="oz nx gu bf ny pa pb dy oc pc pd ea og nh pe pf pg nl ph pi pj np pk pl pm pn bk">ESC6 Requirements in BloodHound</h2><p id="3e25" class="pw-post-body-paragraph mw mx gu my b mz ou nb nc nd ov nf ng nh ow nj nk nl ox nn no np oy nr ns nt gn bk">The base requirement we check for when creating ESC6 edges in BloodHound are:</p><p id="ad3a" class="pw-post-body-paragraph mw mx gu my b mz na nb nc nd ne nf ng nh ni nj nk nl nm nn no np nq nr ns nt gn bk">1) Principal has enrollment rights on a certificate template<br/>2) The certificate template has manager approval disabled<br/>3) The certificate template does not require any authorized signatures<br/>4) The certificate template is published to an enterprise CA<br/>5) Principal has enrollment rights on the enterprise CA<br/>6) The enterprise CA is trusted for NT authentication<br/>7) The enterprise CA’s certificate chain is trusted<br/>8) The enterprise CA’s <code class="cx po pp pq pr b">EDITF_ATTRIBUTESUBJECTALTNAME2</code> flag is enabled</p><p id="abf8" class="pw-post-body-paragraph mw mx gu my b mz na nb nc nd ne nf ng nh ni nj nk nl nm nn no np nq nr ns nt gn bk">Additionally, the requirements for either Kerberos or Schannel authentication must be met.</p><p id="03f3" class="pw-post-body-paragraph mw mx gu my b mz na nb nc nd ne nf ng nh ni nj nk nl nm nn no np nq nr ns nt gn bk">Kerberos authentication:</p><p id="3938" class="pw-post-body-paragraph mw mx gu my b mz na nb nc nd ne nf ng nh ni nj nk nl nm nn no np nq nr ns nt gn bk">9) The certificate template defines EKUs that enable Kerberos authentication<br/>10) The certificate template has the <code class="cx po pp pq pr b">NO_SECURITY_EXTENSION</code> flag</p><p id="fbd0" class="pw-post-body-paragraph mw mx gu my b mz na nb nc nd ne nf ng nh ni nj nk nl nm nn no np nq nr ns nt gn bk">Schannel authentication:</p><p id="020e" class="pw-post-body-paragraph mw mx gu my b mz na nb nc nd ne nf ng nh ni nj nk nl nm nn no np nq nr ns nt gn bk">11) The certificate template defines EKUs that enable Schannel authentication<br/>12) A DC in the forest has Schannel UPN mapping enabled</p><p id="0012" class="pw-post-body-paragraph mw mx gu my b mz na nb nc nd ne nf ng nh ni nj nk nl nm nn no np nq nr ns nt gn bk">The <a class="af nu" rel="noopener ugc nofollow" target="_blank" href="/adcs-attack-paths-in-bloodhound-part-1-799f3d3b03cf">Part 1</a> blog post describes requirement 1–7 in detail and how we have implemented the checks for those in BloodHound, and we went through requirement 9–12 in the section for ESC9 and ESC10.</p><p id="0528" class="pw-post-body-paragraph mw mx gu my b mz na nb nc nd ne nf ng nh ni nj nk nl nm nn no np nq nr ns nt gn bk">Requirement 8 is the only unique requirement for ESC6. For that, we have added a boolean property on EnterpriseCA nodes named <em class="nv">Is User Specifies San Enabled</em> (<code class="cx po pp pq pr b">isuserspecifiessanenabled</code>) which reveals whether the CA has <code class="cx po pp pq pr b">EDITF_ATTRIBUTESUBJECTALTNAME2</code> enabled.</p><h2 id="70a9" class="oz nx gu bf ny pa pb dy oc pc pd ea og nh pe pf pg nl ph pi pj np pk pl pm pn bk">ESC6 — New BloodHound Edges</h2><p id="5cbf" class="pw-post-body-paragraph mw mx gu my b mz ou nb nc nd ov nf ng nh ow nj nk nl ox nn no np oy nr ns nt gn bk">Similarly to the implementation of ESC9 and ESC10, we have divided ESC6 up into two scenarios, this time based on what type of authentication is possible:</p><ul class=""><li id="4892" class="mw mx gu my b mz na nb nc nd ne nf ng nh ni nj nk nl nm nn no np nq nr ns nt qs pt pu bk">ESC6 Scenario A (<a class="af nu" href="https://support.bloodhoundenterprise.io/hc/en-us/articles/22813624300443-ADCSESC6a" rel="noopener ugc nofollow" target="_blank">ADCSESC6a</a>): ESC6 requirements met with Kerberos</li><li id="72ba" class="mw mx gu my b mz pv nb nc nd pw nf ng nh px nj nk nl py nn no np pz nr ns nt qs pt pu bk">ESC6 Scenario B (<a class="af nu" href="https://support.bloodhoundenterprise.io/hc/en-us/articles/22905475276827-ADCSESC6b" rel="noopener ugc nofollow" target="_blank">ADCSESC6b</a>): ESC6 requirements met with Schannel</li></ul><p id="e3ae" class="pw-post-body-paragraph mw mx gu my b mz na nb nc nd ne nf ng nh ni nj nk nl nm nn no np nq nr ns nt gn bk">We have implemented the ADCSESC6 edges in the same way as the other ADCSESCx edges going from the principal that has the privileges to perform the attack towards the domain node of the forest root:</p><figure class="qa qb qc qd qe qf qj qk paragraph-image"><div role="button" tabindex="0" class="qm qn fj qo bh qp"><div class="qj qk rb"><picture><source srcSet="https://miro.medium.com/v2/resize:fit:640/format:webp/0*sYpcNnKosig7ZG3I 640w, https://miro.medium.com/v2/resize:fit:720/format:webp/0*sYpcNnKosig7ZG3I 720w, https://miro.medium.com/v2/resize:fit:750/format:webp/0*sYpcNnKosig7ZG3I 750w, https://miro.medium.com/v2/resize:fit:1100/format:webp/0*sYpcNnKosig7ZG3I 1100w, https://miro.medium.com/v2/resize:fit:1400/format:webp/0*sYpcNnKosig7ZG3I 1400w" sizes="(min-resolution: 4dppx) and (max-width: 700px) 50vw, (-webkit-min-device-pixel-ratio: 4) and (max-width: 700px) 50vw, (min-resolution: 3dppx) and (max-width: 700px) 67vw, (-webkit-min-device-pixel-ratio: 3) and (max-width: 700px) 65vw, (min-resolution: 2.5dppx) and (max-width: 700px) 80vw, (-webkit-min-device-pixel-ratio: 2.5) and (max-width: 700px) 80vw, (min-resolution: 2dppx) and (max-width: 700px) 100vw, (-webkit-min-device-pixel-ratio: 2) and (max-width: 700px) 100vw, 700px" type="image/webp"/><source data-testid="og" srcSet="https://miro.medium.com/v2/resize:fit:640/0*sYpcNnKosig7ZG3I 640w, https://miro.medium.com/v2/resize:fit:720/0*sYpcNnKosig7ZG3I 720w, https://miro.medium.com/v2/resize:fit:750/0*sYpcNnKosig7ZG3I 750w, https://miro.medium.com/v2/resize:fit:786/0*sYpcNnKosig7ZG3I 786w, https://miro.medium.com/v2/resize:fit:828/0*sYpcNnKosig7ZG3I 828w, https://miro.medium.com/v2/resize:fit:1100/0*sYpcNnKosig7ZG3I 1100w, https://miro.medium.com/v2/resize:fit:1400/0*sYpcNnKosig7ZG3I 1400w" sizes="(min-resolution: 4dppx) and (max-width: 700px) 50vw, (-webkit-min-device-pixel-ratio: 4) and (max-width: 700px) 50vw, (min-resolution: 3dppx) and (max-width: 700px) 67vw, (-webkit-min-device-pixel-ratio: 3) and (max-width: 700px) 65vw, (min-resolution: 2.5dppx) and (max-width: 700px) 80vw, (-webkit-min-device-pixel-ratio: 2.5) and (max-width: 700px) 80vw, (min-resolution: 2dppx) and (max-width: 700px) 100vw, (-webkit-min-device-pixel-ratio: 2) and (max-width: 700px) 100vw, 700px"/><img alt="" class="bh md qq c" width="700" height="194" loading="lazy" role="presentation"/></picture></div></div></figure><p id="5887" class="pw-post-body-paragraph mw mx gu my b mz na nb nc nd ne nf ng nh ni nj nk nl nm nn no np nq nr ns nt gn bk">The ESC6 edges have a composition graph that reveals the components involved in the same manner as the other ADCSESCx edges.</p><h1 id="bed9" class="nw nx gu bf ny nz oa ob oc od oe of og oh oi oj ok ol om on oo op oq or os ot bk">DC Registry Collection</h1><p id="2001" class="pw-post-body-paragraph mw mx gu my b mz ou nb nc nd ov nf ng nh ow nj nk nl ox nn no np oy nr ns nt gn bk">We have added support in SharpHound for collecting the <code class="cx po pp pq pr b">StrongCertificateBindingEnforcement</code> and <code class="cx po pp pq pr b">CertificateMappingMethods</code> registry values from DCs with the <em class="nv">DCRegistry</em> collection method. BloodHound will not produce any ESC6 Scenario B, ESC9, or ESC10 edges without the registry values collected.</p><p id="e490" class="pw-post-body-paragraph mw mx gu my b mz na nb nc nd ne nf ng nh ni nj nk nl nm nn no np nq nr ns nt gn bk">However, the account running SharpHound must have admin rights on the DCs to collect this information. As a defender, you might be able to run SharpHound with those privileges, but it is likely not an option if you are a red teamer or penetration tester.</p><p id="4998" class="pw-post-body-paragraph mw mx gu my b mz na nb nc nd ne nf ng nh ni nj nk nl nm nn no np nq nr ns nt gn bk">As an alternative, you can manually replace the <code class="cx po pp pq pr b">DCRegistryData</code> JSON object in the SharpHound computers.json output file under a DC node to assume it meets the requirements for weak certificate mapping for both Kerberos and Schannel:</p><pre class="qa qb qc qd qe rc pr rd bp re bb bk"><span id="7d81" class="rf nx gu pr b bg rg rh l ri rj">&quot;DCRegistryData&quot;: {<br/>  &quot;CertificateMappingMethods&quot;: {<br/>    &quot;Value&quot;: 31,<br/>    &quot;Collected&quot;: true,<br/>    &quot;FailureReason&quot;: null<br/>  },<br/>  &quot;StrongCertificateBindingEnforcement&quot;: {<br/>    &quot;Value&quot;: 1,<br/>    &quot;Collected&quot;: true,<br/>    &quot;FailureReason&quot;: null<br/>  }<br/>},</span></pre><p id="2a05" class="pw-post-body-paragraph mw mx gu my b mz na nb nc nd ne nf ng nh ni nj nk nl nm nn no np nq nr ns nt gn bk">You can also assume default configuration by changing the 31 integer to 24.</p><p id="e450" class="pw-post-body-paragraph mw mx gu my b mz na nb nc nd ne nf ng nh ni nj nk nl nm nn no np nq nr ns nt gn bk">Alternatively, you can use some of the predefined queries covered in the next section to find ESC6/9/10 opportunities without ADCSESCx edges.</p><h1 id="796b" class="nw nx gu bf ny nz oa ob oc od oe of og oh oi oj ok ol om on oo op oq or os ot bk">Predefined Cypher Queries</h1><p id="cd95" class="pw-post-body-paragraph mw mx gu my b mz ou nb nc nd ov nf ng nh ow nj nk nl ox nn no np oy nr ns nt gn bk">We have a handful of new predefined cypher queries that can help you find interesting things related to ESC6/9/10:</p><ul class=""><li id="b707" class="mw mx gu my b mz na nb nc nd ne nf ng nh ni nj nk nl nm nn no np nq nr ns nt qs pt pu bk">Enrollment rights on published certificate templates with no security extension</li><li id="319b" class="mw mx gu my b mz pv nb nc nd pw nf ng nh px nj nk nl py nn no np pz nr ns nt qs pt pu bk">Enrollment rights on certificate templates published to Enterprise CA with User Specified SAN enabled <em class="nv">(ESC6)</em></li><li id="e411" class="mw mx gu my b mz pv nb nc nd pw nf ng nh px nj nk nl py nn no np pz nr ns nt qs pt pu bk">Domain controllers with weak certificate binding enabled <em class="nv">(Kerberos)</em></li><li id="390b" class="mw mx gu my b mz pv nb nc nd pw nf ng nh px nj nk nl py nn no np pz nr ns nt qs pt pu bk">Domain controllers with UPN certificate mapping enabled <em class="nv">(Schannel)</em></li></ul><figure class="qa qb qc qd qe qf qj qk paragraph-image"><div role="button" tabindex="0" class="qm qn fj qo bh qp"><div class="qj qk rk"><picture><source ob oc od oe of og oh oi oj ok ol om on oo op oq or os ot bk">Remediation</h1><p id="98d3" class="pw-post-body-paragraph mw mx gu my b mz ou nb nc nd ov nf ng nh ow nj nk nl ox nn no np oy nr ns nt gn bk">You are a defender and you have discovered (or been made aware) that your AD environment has ADCS ESC6/9/10 attack paths. Great...</p><figure class="qa qb qc qd qe qf qj qk paragraph-image"><div class="qj qk rl"><picture><source srcSet="https://miro.medium.com/v2/resize:fit:640/format:webp/0*-K4TIOGF0C3qrW1e 640w, https://miro.medium.com/v2/resize:fit:1100/format:webp/0*-K4TIOGF0C3qrW1e 1100w, https://miro.medium.com/v2/resize:fit:1036/format:webp/0*-K4TIOGF0C3qrW1e 1036w" sizes="(min-resolution: 4dppx) and (max-width: 700px) 50vw, (-webkit-min-device-pixel-ratio: 4) and (max-width: 700px) 50vw, (min-resolution: 3dppx) and (max-width: 700px) 67vw, (-webkit-min-device-pixel-ratio: 3) and (max-width: (-webkit-min-device-pixel-ratio: 2) and (max-width: 700px) 100vw, 518px" type="image/webp"/><source data-testid="og" srcSet="https://miro.medium.com/v2/resize:fit:640/0*-K4TIOGF0C3qrW1e 640w, https://miro.medium.com/v2/resize:fit:720/0*-K4TIOGF0C3qrW1e 720w, https://miro.medium.com/v2/resize:fit:750/0*-K4TIOGF0C3qrW1e 750w, https://miro.medium.com/v2/resize:fit:786/0*-K4TIOGF0C3qrW1e 786w, https://miro.medium.com/v2/resize:fit:828/0*-K4TIOGF0C3qrW1e 828w, https://miro.medium.com/v2/resize:fit:1100/0*-K4TIOGF0C3qrW1e 1100w, https://miro.medium.com/v2/resize:fit:1036/0*-K4TIOGF0C3qrW1e 1036w" sizes="(min-resolution: 4dppx) and (max-width: 700px) 50vw, (-webkit-min-device-pixel-ratio: 4) and (max-width: 700px) 50vw, (min-resolution: 3dppx) and (max-width: 700px) 67vw, (-webkit-min-device-pixel-ratio: 3) and (max-width: 700px) 65vw, (min-resolution: 2.5dppx) and (max-width: 700px) 80vw, (-webkit-min-device-pixel-ratio: 2.5) and (max-width: 700px) 80vw, (min-resolution: 2dppx) and (max-width: 700px) 100vw, (-webkit-min-device-pixel-ratio: 2) and (max-width: 700px) 100vw, 518px"/><img alt="" class="bh md qq c" width="518" height="402" loading="lazy" role="presentation"/></picture></div></figure><h2 id="cbbc" class="oz nx gu bf ny pa pb dy oc pc pd ea og nh pe pf pg nl ph pi pj np pk pl pm pn bk">ESC6 Remediation</h2><p id="166d" class="pw-post-body-paragraph mw mx gu my b mz ou nb nc nd ov nf ng nh ow nj nk nl ox nn no np oy nr ns nt gn bk">You should get rid of the <code class="cx po pp pq pr b">EDITF_ATTRIBUTESUBJECTALTNAME2</code> flag on your enterprise CAs. But first, try to get a clear understanding of the current usage of the functionality enabled by the flag so you do not break the environment.</p><p id="97cc" class="pw-post-body-paragraph mw mx gu my b mz na nb nc nd ne nf ng nh ni nj nk nl nm nn no np nq nr ns nt gn bk"><strong class="my gv">1) Identify </strong><code class="cx po pp pq pr b"><strong class="my gv">EDITF_ATTRIBUTESUBJECTALTNAME2</strong></code><strong class="my gv"> Usage</strong></p><p id="d623" class="pw-post-body-paragraph mw mx gu my b mz na nb nc nd ne nf ng nh ni nj nk nl nm nn no np nq nr ns nt gn bk">When a principal creates a certificate request that is supposed to use the <code class="cx po pp pq pr b">EDITF_ATTRIBUTESUBJECTALTNAME2</code> feature, then the certificate request incorporates the SAN name with X509 name/value pairs. This is different from Enrollee Supplies Subject (ESC1) requests where an X509 extension is used, and it therefore allows us to distinguish between the two types.</p><p id="fe50" class="pw-post-body-paragraph mw mx gu my b mz na nb nc nd ne nf ng nh ni nj nk nl nm nn no np nq nr ns nt gn bk">You can use <a class="af nu" href="https://github.com/GhostPack/PSPKIAudit" rel="noopener ugc nofollow" target="_blank">PSPKIAudit</a>’s <code class="cx po pp pq pr b">Get-CertRequest</code> function as a CA Administrator to identify certificate requests utilizing the <code class="cx po pp pq pr b">EDITF_ATTRIBUTESUBJECTALTNAME2</code> feature:</p><pre class="qa qb qc qd qe rc pr rd bp re bb bk"><span id="8669" class="rf nx gu pr b bg rg rh l ri rj"><strong class="pr gv">PS C:\&gt; Get-CertRequest -CAName dumpster-DC01-CA -HasSAN | ? {$_.SubjectAltNamesAttrib -ne &quot;&quot;}</strong><br/><br/>CA : DC01.dumpster.fire\dumpster-DC01-CA<br/>RequestID : 180<br/>RequesterName : DUMPSTER\jd<br/>RequesterMachineName : DC01.dumpster.fire<br/>RequesterProcessName : Certify.exe<br/>SubjectAltNamesExtension : Roshi<br/>SubjectAltNamesAttrib : Roshi<br/>SerialNumber : 7b000000b4822ab66661d867a10000000000b4<br/>CertificateTemplate : ClientAuth2 (1.3.6.1.4.1.311.21.8.4571196.1884641.3293620.10686285.12068043.134.3767878.1854842)<br/>RequestDate : 12/13/2023 7:23:13 PM<br/>StartDate : 12/13/2023 7:13:13 PM<br/>EndDate : 12/12/2024 7:13:13 PM</span></pre><p id="11aa" class="pw-post-body-paragraph mw mx gu my b mz na nb nc nd ne nf ng nh ni nj nk nl nm nn no np nq nr ns nt gn bk">These requests can help you determine which users (and from what computers) are utilizing the feature. You can ignore certificate requests with an expired end date, and also requests where the requester name refers to the same account as the <code class="cx po pp pq pr b">SubjectAltNamesAttrib</code> property.</p><p id="9dcd" class="pw-post-body-paragraph mw mx gu my b mz na nb nc nd ne nf ng nh ni nj nk nl nm nn no np nq nr ns nt gn bk"><strong class="my gv">2) Implement a More Secure Solution</strong></p><p id="d84c" class="pw-post-body-paragraph mw mx gu my b mz na nb nc nd ne nf ng nh ni nj nk nl nm nn no np nq nr ns nt gn bk">The most secure feature allowing the creation of certificates as other accounts is <em class="nv">enrollment agents</em> (ESC3). You can use enrollment agent restrictions to configure which accounts a given enrollment agent can enroll as, preventing unintended domain escalation scenarios.</p><p id="6f32" class="pw-post-body-paragraph mw mx gu my b mz na nb nc nd ne nf ng nh ni nj nk nl nm nn no np nq nr ns nt gn bk">Alternatively, you can configure a certificate template with the Enrollee Supplies Subject flag (ESC1). This allows the enrollees to enroll as anyone, which is why you must restrict enrollment rights to Tier Zero accounts on such a template.</p><p id="41d5" class="pw-post-body-paragraph mw mx gu my b mz na nb nc nd ne nf ng nh ni nj nk nl nm nn no np nq nr ns nt gn bk"><strong class="my gv">3) Remove </strong><code class="cx po pp pq pr b"><strong class="my gv">EDITF_ATTRIBUTESUBJECTALTNAME2</strong></code><strong class="my gv"> flag</strong></p><p id="ca4a" class="pw-post-body-paragraph mw mx gu my b mz na nb nc nd ne nf ng nh ni nj nk nl nm nn no np nq nr ns nt gn bk">Log in on the enterprise CA server as CA Administrator (or member of the Administrators on the host) and open CMD as Administrator. Check whether the CA has EDITF_ATTRIBUTESUBJECALTNAME2 enabled:</p><pre class="qa qb qc qd qe rc pr rd bp re bb bk"><span id="d7d0" class="rf nx gu pr b bg rg rh l ri rj">certutil -getreg policy\EditFlags</span></pre><p id="ed7b" class="pw-post-body-paragraph mw mx gu my b mz na nb nc nd ne nf ng nh ni nj nk nl nm nn no np nq nr ns nt gn bk">The CA has If <code class="cx po pp pq pr b">EDITF_ATTRIBUTESUBJECTALTNAME2</code> enabled if the output includes the flag. To disable the setting, run the following command:</p><pre class="qa qb qc qd qe rc pr rd bp re bb bk"><span id="1565" class="rf nx gu pr b bg rg rh l ri rj">certutil -setreg policy\EditFlags -EDITF_ATTRIBUTESUBJECTALTNAME2</span></pre><p id="8192" class="pw-post-body-paragraph mw mx gu my b mz na nb nc nd ne nf ng nh ni nj nk nl nm nn no np nq nr ns nt gn bk">Then restart the CA service:</p><pre class="qa qb qc qd qe rc pr rd bp re bb bk"><span id="dcc3" class="rf nx gu pr b bg rg rh l ri rj">net stop certsvc &amp;&amp; net start certsvc</span></pre><p id="26b1" class="pw-post-body-paragraph mw mx gu my b mz na nb nc nd ne nf ng nh ni nj nk nl nm nn no np nq nr ns nt gn bk">If you need to enable the flag again, then run this command followed by a restart of the CA service:</p><pre class="qa qb qc qd qe rc pr rd bp re bb bk"><span id="2cd1" class="rf nx gu pr b bg rg rh l ri rj">certutil -setreg policy\EditFlags +EDITF_ATTRIBUTESUBJECTALTNAME2</span></pre><h2 id="6b2d" class="oz nx gu bf ny pa pb dy oc pc pd ea og nh pe pf pg nl ph pi pj np pk pl pm pn bk">ESC9 and ESC10 Remediation</h2><p id="3dc3" class="pw-post-body-paragraph mw mx gu my b mz ou nb nc nd ov nf ng nh ow nj nk nl ox nn no np oy nr ns nt gn bk">Check out the <a class="af nu" href="https://support.microsoft.com/en-us/topic/kb5014754-certificate-based-authentication-changes-on-windows-domain-controllers-ad2c23b0-15d8-4340-a468-4d4f3b188f16" rel="noopener ugc nofollow" target="_blank">KB5014754: Certificate-based authentication changes on Windows domain controllers</a> article from Microsoft. It provides helpful information about strong certificate mapping enforcement and how you can use event IDs to track down accounts and systems that are not compliant.</p><h1 id="6f5f" class="nw nx gu bf ny nz oa ob oc od oe of og oh oi oj ok ol om on oo op oq or os ot bk">ESC6/9/10 in BloodHound Enterprise</h1><p id="6d24" class="pw-post-body-paragraph mw mx gu my b mz ou nb nc nd ov nf ng nh ow nj nk nl ox nn no np oy nr ns nt gn bk">The six new edges are all traversable. That means we generate a finding for every non-Tier Zero principal that has any of the edges in BloodHound Enterprise so you can become easily aware of the risk:</p><figure class="qa qb qc qd qe qf qj qk paragraph-image"><div role="button" tabindex="0" class="qm qn fj qo bh qp"><div class="qj qk rk"><picture><source srcSet="https://miro.medium.com/v2/resize:fit:640/format:webp/0*QC93UpOKZhuk0I3F (-webkit-min-device-pixel-ratio: 2) and (max-width: 700px) 100vw, 700px" type="image/webp"/><source data-testid="og" srcSet="https://miro.medium.com/v2/resize:fit:640/0*QC93UpOKZhuk0I3F 640w, https://miro.medium.com/v2/resize:fit:720/0*QC93UpOKZhuk0I3F 720w, https://miro.medium.com/v2/resize:fit:750/0*QC93UpOKZhuk0I3F 750w, https://miro.medium.com/v2/resize:fit:786/0*QC93UpOKZhuk0I3F 786w, include a full remediation plan.</p><p id="780e" class="pw-post-body-paragraph mw mx gu my b mz na nb nc nd ne nf ng nh ni nj nk nl nm nn no np nq nr ns nt gn bk">Everything else covered in this blog post is available in both BloodHound Community Edition (CE) and Enterprise.</p><p id="9a1d" class="pw-post-body-paragraph mw mx gu my b mz na nb nc nd ne nf ng nh ni nj nk nl nm nn no np nq nr ns nt gn bk">If you are interested in learning more about the features of BloodHound Enterprise then check out the <a class="af nu" href="https://specterops.io/bloodhound-feature-comparison/" rel="noopener ugc nofollow" target="_blank">comparison of CE and Enterprise</a> or schedule an informational <a class="af nu" href="https://specterops.io/get-a-demo/" rel="noopener ugc nofollow" target="_blank">BloodHound Enterprise demo</a> with us.</p><h1 id="726e" class="nw nx gu bf ny nz oa ob oc od oe of og oh oi oj ok ol om on oo op oq or os ot bk">What is Next</h1><p id="39fd" class="pw-post-body-paragraph mw mx gu my b mz ou nb nc nd ov nf ng nh ow nj nk nl ox nn no np oy nr ns nt gn bk">We have now covered ESC1, Golden Certificate, ESC3, ESC6, ESC9, and ESC10 with this blog post series. Stay tuned for future posts, as we will dive further into more ADCS escalations and how you can identify them using BloodHound.</p><p id="6fb6" class="pw-post-body-paragraph mw mx gu my b mz na nb nc nd ne nf ng nh ni nj nk nl nm nn no np nq nr ns nt gn bk">We are eager to hear your feedback.
